B00C3V5TTQ|5.0|I will admit, I am not a huge OMD fan - I like roughly a quarter of their material. Everything from the debut record through Dazzle Ships to be exact. I prefer their melodic, minimal Kraftwerk-influenced analog sounds opposed to their more commercial pop releases thereafter. I'm much more a Depeche Mode fan, preferring the darker melodies etc.<br /><br />With that being said, when History of Modern came out with the hype of being comparable to their earliest records, I was on board. Sadly, the only track I liked was Sister Mary Says - not surprising since that song was written in the band's early days. Every other track on the album was marred either by poor lyrics or just mediocre songwriting.<br /><br />I stayed optimistic when I heard English Electric was coming. When Metroland was released, I was immediately hooked and I bought the whole record.<br /><br />All I can say is wow. They fixed EVERY error they made on History of Modern. Amazing old school synth sounds, sugary warm vocal melodies and catchy rhythms. Minus the Kraftwerk-like instrumentals, you are left with 8 tracks and 37 minutes of music. All of it gold. As much as the lyrics are about a dystopian future, I can't help but feel uplifted by this album. My favorites are of course Metroland, Night Cafe, and Stay With Me. I'm going to have this on heavy rotation for sure.<br /><br />A lot of people are saying it, and I'm saying it too - this is OMD's best record, and coming 33 years after their debut, thats incredible.<br /><br />Here is to a few more great records before they call it a day. Got to love the Peter Saville cover art that screams Factory Records.<br /><br />Must have Synthpop album of 2013.

0060936223|4.0|Just Kids by Patti Smith is a rare little gem. To me, Patti Smith has always exuded a punk rock swagger, an only half-bridled aggression. I see her, and I see only the hard, sharp angles of her. And Robert Mapplethorpe: leather, whips, unapologetic sex acts with a peculiar defiant dignity to them. Both of them are creatures who seem to have been launched straight from the scene, fully-formed and antagonistic right from the start.<br /><br />But they weren't. Just Kids is a memoir by Patti Smith about her time living in New York with Robert Mapplethorpe while they were both shaking off the dull scraps of adolescence and trying to break out as artists. Strewn throughout the book are pictures of them as very young excitable artists-in-training joined at the hip.<br /><br />Smith's prose reads like a soft-focus fairy tale. The sections set in the Chelsea Hotel, especially, have an almost Dickensian quality to them; they read as a quaint story full of larger-than-life characters, most of whom have hearts firmly of gold. Reconciling this wistful retelling of her youth with the persona I associate with her was intriguing to say the least. And obviously I am not the only one who found the disconnect between Patti Smith's presence and her internal life jarring - there are places in the text where she discusses how those around her took her for a lesbian (she is straight), or a junkie (she seems not to have experimented with pot until she'd moved out of the Chelsea). Her prose is light and airy, and her memories sepia-tinged and wholesome, despite the fact that anyone who knows the history of that scene knows just how much death and self-immolation is happening just off screen. Patti Smith herself seems to have waltzed through it unscathed, and her writing dances along the edges of the darkness that her scene held*. Without the debauchery, the excess, the Chelsea Hotel in the 70s reads as an almost Victorian affair.<br /><br />The book is structured in a circle: it opens with the moment Smith hears of Mapplethorpe's death, then jumps back in time before they have met. Smith discusses her teenage pregnancy and the process of giving her child up for adoption, her failure at teacher's school, and her time on a New Jersey assembly line in a brisk and somewhat sanitized fashion; again, there seems to be in her writing a distaste for discussions of the negative, of the hard and bleak moments of her life. From there, the book jumps forward to her first meeting with Mapplethorpe, their sweet and heartfelt romance, the little poverty-stricken life they build together, and how hard they worked to evolve their relationship with each other when their life trajectories began to diverge. The book ends with a far jump into the future, back to those last few weeks of Mapplethorpe's life and ends with his inevitable death, right back where the book started.<br /><br />Given that the book is told from Smith's perspective, it is perhaps not surprising that her motives and desires are clear throughout, but over the course of the book Mapplethorpe becomes more and more opaque. A boy who seems simple when she first meets him grows into a man full of contradictions. The person whose viewpoints and life goals seemed to mirror hers so closely at first winds up yearning to be part of the social circles that Smith herself actively avoids. It became increasingly unsettling as I read the book. What does he get from her that keeps him around? How does he see her and their ever-changing relationship? Very little is explored here in the text, and Smith herself seems to take their relationship at face value, as a thing complete in itself with little context surrounding it. It just is for her, and her wholesale acceptance of it is so radically different from the way I, personally, live out my significant life-altering relationships that it was hard for me to understand at times what their relationship was exactly. But there is an authenticity to her writing that explains the halcyon haze through which she remembers that time of her life. That period, above anything else, was her period with Robert Mapplethorpe, for whom she had a love so total and accepting it is essentially blank, without specificities, and all the hardness of that time is drowned out in remembrance of him.<br /><br />Just Kids is, like most memoirs, ultimately a work that says as much or more about its author than the subject matter itself. The story there is as much in the telling as it is in the content. And it's a fascinating look into the mind of a woman who is so very different than the person I assumed her to be. It is a love letter to the late Robert Mapplethorpe, but it's a love letter to her young self, as well. I can't help but wish there had been some balance to it, some acknowledgment of the difficulties of living so poor, or of loving a man who seems to fall into and out of and into and out of love with her, or of the pain of watching her friends get consumed by drugs right in front of her, but that's not the book she wrote. It may not be a book she's able to write. I can't help but think of her as an unreliable narrator for her own life, but ultimately that's what we all are. It's hard to tell the bald truth about your own life. It might be impossible. But still, the unanswered questions nag at me. I found this book absolutely fascinating, but when it was over, it felt insubstantial. But that doesn't mean it's not worth reading.<br /><br />*Swimming Underground by Mary Woronov of Warhol's Factory crew is a bird's eye account of the dark addictions Patti Smith seems to prefer to keep just out of frame. I highly recommend it, too, and it works as a very interesting counterbalance to Just Kids.s the halcyon haze through which she remembers that time of her life. That period, above anything else, was her period with Robert Mapplethorpe, for whom she had a love so total and accepting it is essentially blank, without specificities, and all the hardness of that time is drowned out in remembrance of him.<br /><br />Just Kids is, like most memoirs, ultimately a work that says as much or more about its author than the subject matter itself. The story there is as much in the telling as it is in the content. And it's a fascinating look into the mind of a woman who is so very different than the person I assumed her to be. It is a love letter to the late Robert Mapplethorpe, but it's a love letter to her young self, as well. I can't help but wish there had been some balance to it, some acknowledgment of the difficulties of living so poor, or of loving a man who seems to fall into and out of and into and out of love with her, or of the pain of watching her friends get consumed by drugs right in front of her, but that's not the book she wrote. 0307719219|4.0|Why Nations Fail is a book that everyone should read and think about. However the theory proposed by the authors is flawed. The authors are professors in economics and government and thus it is no surprise that they conclude that nations fail because of political and economic institutions.  When the only tool you have is a hammer, everything begins to look like a nail.<br /><br />To begin with they fail to define their terms, specifically \\"fail\\" and \\"sustainable.\\"  Their hypothesis is that nations fail because they have \\"extractive\\" political institutions that in turn lead to extractive economic institutions, Conversely, nations succeed when they have \\"inclusive\\" political institutions that result in inclusive economic institutions.  In other words, democracy leads to capitalism which is good and authoritarianism leads to economic stagnation which is bad.  Furthermore, this view assumes that every authoritarian leader and government is out to get what it can for the elites and ignore the rest of the people and every democratic leader wants to provide for the people. This view ignores people such as Lee Kuan Yew in Singapore, an authoritarian leader who clearly had the best interests of his people at heart and even King Abdullah in Saudi Arabia who has undertaken a number of efforts for people in his country.<br /><br />They begin with an interesting example. After Columbus discovered the New World it was the Spanish who were in the best position to take advantage and Spanish conquistadors came largely to Central and South America and employed the strategy of capturing the local chief and forcing him to give them gold and other resources.  After the defeat of the Spanish Armada Great Britain had the opportunity to come to the Americas but had to settle for what was left over, namely the coast of North America. The British initially tried the Spanish strategy but failed because the local chiefs were too strong and wary and in any case there was no gold. The initial colony at Jamestown of 500 dwindled to 50. At that point the British tried coercion imposing the British manorial system on the remaining colonists. A lord was appointed and given the land, which the colonists were supposed, to farm as tenants, but that plan did not work either because the colonists could just go up the road a piece and start a new colony.  Finally, since nothing else worked the British tried incentives--political and economic inclusion and voila! the future Silicon Valley was born.  Well it is a nice story.  But there is more.<br /><br />Why did the Industrial Revolution start in Britain and not elsewhere?  The answer is the Glorious Revolution (in which William of Orange and Mary took over rule in Britain from James II thus moving the country from an extractive political system to an inclusive one which led to an inclusive economic system and success followed in due course.<br /><br />There is certain much to be said in favor of these ideas, but they do not represent the whole picture of why nations succeed or fail.  The authors disparage Jared Diamond's view that geography (and natural resources) plays a significant part in the success or failure of a country. But the reality is that the United States, for example, has benefited greatly from its geographic position, protected on both sides by wide oceans and north and south by weak neighbors and its abundance of natural resources, Similarly they discredit Max Weber's view that the Protestant Ethic was a major factor in Europe's success, noting that many successful Asian countries are not Christian. True but the mentality (work hard and strive for success) is not merely a Protestant phenomenon.  They also fail to adequately take into account the influence of specific leaders, the so-called \\"Great Man' theory. Human beings affect outcomes including and especially political and economic institutions. Would WW II have occurred if there was no Hitler? Would the British have resisted as well without Churchill? What would Russia have done without Stalin or for that matter what would Russia have been without Lenin?  Instead they refer to \\"small differences and critical junctures.\\"<br /><br />Extractive countries such as China, Russia and Saudi Arabia may be successful now but this success is \\"not sustainable.\\"  But nothing lasts forever and the inclusive countries of Europe are now in a severe economic crisis while the extractive BRIC countries continue to prosper. And what about Singapore, authoritarian yet hardly extractive and quite successful? In Saudi Arabia last year the non-natural resource part of the economy grew faster than the resource part and while King Abdullah and his family extract they also give substantially.  Then there is India, until recently a failure, yet strongly democratic.<br /><br />For that matter how inclusive is the United States? Obama came to the San Francisco Bay Area recently and stayed at the home of a billionaire and spent his time with other wealthy people. I live here but needless to say he had no interest in meeting me!<br /><br />In sum, Why Nations Fail is a book that will challenge and inform you and get you to thinking about the world and how it is changing.  The subject is complex and not definable by one paradigm.chill? What would Russia have done without Stalin or for that matter what would Russia have been without Lenin?  Instead they refer to \\"small differences and critical junctures.\\"<br /><br />Extractive countries such as China, Russia and Saudi Arabia may be successful now but this success is \\"not sustainable.\\"  But nothing lasts forever and the inclusive countries of Europe are now in a severe economic crisis while the extractive BRIC countries continue to prosper. And what about Singapore, authoritarian yet hardly extractive and quite successful? In Saudi Arabia last year the non-natural resource part of the economy grew faster than the resource part and while King Abdullah and his family extract they also give substantially.  Then there is India, until recently a failure, yet strongly democratic.<br /><br />For that matter how inclusive is the United States? Obama came to the San Francisco Bay Area recently and stayed at the home of a billionaire and spent his time with other wealthy people. I live here but needless to say he had no interest in meeting me!<br /><br />In sum, Why Nations Fail is a book that will challenge and inform you and get you to thinking about the world and how it is changing.  The subject is complex and not definable by one paradigm.

B000E41MRC|5.0|Wikipedia has a good definition of \\"groupthink\\":  \\"Group members try to minimize conflict and reach a consensus decision without critical evaluation of alternative ideas or viewpoints, and by isolating themselves from outside influences.\\"  To learn how the Nazis gained an \\"us against the world\\" mentality, watch this propaganda documentary of the 1934 Nuremberg rally.  You will notice in the speeches that emotionalism overtakes rationality and that Hitler, while anything but good-looking (I still do not see how he could seem sexy to women, even with the huge media campaign trying to make him so), did have huge charisma as a speaker, specializing in starting calmly and working up into a frenzy.  The bigness of the event and sense of unity and stability were supposed to make Nazism look really good.<br /><br />It is interesting to try to understand the contradiction in Nazi ideology--classnessness (the wealthy and the poor all have important roles in the new Germany), but elitism and hateful racism.  The extent to which the swastika was worshipped can only be appreciated when you see a huge Nazi rally like on this documentary.  The swastika represented the progression of history in Nazi terms:  people dying for Germany (there's no afterlife), and then future generations appreciating that sacrifice and again dying for Germany, to help it expand through the world.  There was no further justification of why German culture was superior, other than propaganda films showing other races looking ugly.  I was surprised by the lack of rationale given to the Nazis at the Nuremberg rally in this film.  They don't learn WHY furthering the master-race/Fatherland matters, or why they're supposedly superior to the rest of the world; it's irrelevant.<br /><br />This documentary preserves the aura of splendor that Hitler originally had, and gives much food for thought about the danger of groupthink.  Germany had a strong military tradition and unsuccessful experiment with democracy in the 20s (it wasn't able to save them from the Great Depression), so it was not as hard for Germany to accept Hitler as it would be for America.  An economic crisis can do a lot to make people willing to relinquish freedom.  Hitler's humanistic optimism about the ability of German youth to perfect Germany, and defense of his power by saying the German people wanted/supported him, are repeated dangerously today by the Democratic Party.  His populism's only moral defense was that the people were helped economically and that the Aryan race was best served by an unwavering leader like himself.  He also had a skill at making sure people didn't get JUST a message of hate toward foreigners (especially Jews), but a positive message of German pride as well.<br /><br />Sometimes you can best detect a dictator by what he doesn't say, not just what he does say.  Hitler never emphasized his subservience to the people (even if he acknowledged it sometimes, it was not EMPHASIZED), or expressed a sense of humility before God for his moral choices.  Listening to the speeches on this documentary is a good way to learn how a demagogue, manipulator of the people, works:  emotionalism, humanistic optimism which ignores the need for God or accountability to God for our moral choices, and flattering the people.  I suppose Nazi racism was at least partially motivated by the need to make the voter base feel more important, so the Jews had to be made \\"the enemy\\" in the public's eyes.

0785139168|4.0|I never thought that I would see the day a classic of literature would be adapted into comic book format, much less that I would greatly enjoy this type of adaption. I mean, I love comics, but this type of comic book? Can it possibly be adapted well at all? The answer is a definite yes.<br /><br />In Marvel Comics' adaptation of Jane Austen's beloved classic Pride and Prejudice, the story was presented in it's most essential points. Adapted for comic book format by Nancy Butler and Hugo Petrus, much of the story in the original novel was interpreted from the thoughts of the characters to showing, via the artwork, what was occuring. So, naturally, some of the parts of the book were dropped in favor of the most important plot points.<br /><br />Really, this is not a huge problem for me, as this is a necessity for any adaption, comic or otherwise. You simply can not adapt everything perfectly, even if you try to do so. Sometimes, in order to convey the gist of what happens in a tale, you have to change, or even add to, the story. For instance, though it was trashed for adding too many scenes, the Disney and Walden Media adaptation of The Chronicles of Narnia: The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe added material to show the rivalry between Peter and Edmund, to illustrate the point that CS Lewis stresses, that Edmund isn't as bad as he appears to be. Also, the air raids, well known from experiences or pictures to the audience when the books were first written, were shown to bring the point home to modern audiences. That is only that movie, however. The other two Narnia films from Walden Media were trash, in my opinion.<br /><br />The point of the above paragraph is to illustrate just what type of \\"changes\\" in the story were brought to bear in this comic adaptation. Basically, it's a case of showing instead of telling. Instead of having long paragraphs telling what the characters are thinking, they show it via their facial expressions, so on.<br /><br />Really, this was the second-best adaptation I have seen of P & P, right below the 1995 BBC mini-series starring Jennifer Ehle and Colin Firthe. It was very, very faithful to the story, and gave you the same gist as the original novel. None of this modern penchant of making any of the three sisters of Jane and Lizzie look better was there, and none of absolving either Darcy or Lizzie. These are two mistakes in adapting this story that occur far too often.<br /><br />The only real criticism I have of this graphic novel adaptation of P & P is the art, or rather one aspect thereof. Don't get me wrong, the art was gorgeous, and actually made things look like they would have looked (for the most part) at that time in the late 18th/early 19th century. The problem with the art was how it made the characters look. It seems that the artists of comic books can not draw anyone looking average or slightly attractive. Instead, everyone must look either stunningly beautiful, or horrifically ugly. In this story, literally EVERYONE was stunning except for Mrs Bennett (the selfish mother), who was made out to be way too unappealing, to say the least. They were drawn in such as way that was out of line with the novel. I wish these comic book folks would draw average looking folks.<br /><br />Of course, it isn't that bad that this is all that I can critique in this adaptation, and I heartily recommend it for any fans of comic books, the Classics, or both.e second-best adaptation I have seen of P & P, right below the 1995 BBC mini-series starring Jennifer Ehle and Colin Firthe. 0719079276|5.0|I didn't discover this book until halfway through a graduate level class on Postcolonial Theory. I wish I'd read it years ago.<br /><br />Despite its bland title and intimidating chapter headings, this book is very accessible. A brief history of each theory is given, along with an introduction to important scholars and influential works in the field. For me, the most helpful aspect was the practical application: the bullet pointed lists of what scholars from each camp actually do, and the mini-essays interpreting specific poems and short stories using the principles of each camp of literary criticism. (Several poems and short stories are included in the appendix and it was fascinating to see the same story interpreted differently depending on the chapter.)<br /><br />For me, reading this book was like discovering the cipher to a code that I was already supposed to know. The articles for my class, which are littered with references to Foucault, textual deconstruction, Bhabha, and Narratology, are beginning to make sense. The genius of this book is its ability to take obtuse terms and abstract concepts and to bring them down to earth. The author writes in an easy going, casual voice, and isn't afraid to poke fun at his own profession at times. After Derrida, it's a breath of fresh air.

0143120530|5.0|I was a little reluctant to pick up this book. I thought it might be just an account of techniques that could be used to memorize lists of things. Since I knew I probably wouldn't be interested in doing that, I hesitated. I'm glad I decided in favor of \\"Moonwalking\\" though. It's one of the best books I've read this year.<br /><br />Author Joshua Foer does relate some of the techniques that \\"memory champs\\" use to recall strings of numbers, long poems, people's names, etc. He briefly outlines a few of the methods that the ancient Greeks and Romans used, and that modern memory gurus such as Tony Buzan have re-packaged and presented to audiences worldwide. There is the \\"Memory Palace,\\" a place you visualize and in which you hang bizarre images that translate to numbers or names. There are ways of equating strings of numbers with letters that you then compose into words.<br /><br />But this book is so much more than an account of mnemonic techniques. It is a thoughtful, well-written discussion of the way memory shapes our perception of time, and of who we are as individuals and as humans. He gives the reasons we might all wish to exert ourselves to remember more.<br /><br />This leads into any number of fascinating topics along the way. Foer reconstructs how writing arose and how for hundreds of years it was not meant to be an external memory, but simply a way to jog our internal memories. He considers subjects such as what constitutes a \\"good\\" education. Rote memory is now anathema to progressive educators. Have they been right in adopting Dewey's philosophy of experiencing rather than knowing?<br /><br />What is it like to be totally amnesiac for anything more recent than 50 years ago? Foer does some of his best writing in describing the predicament of such a man he interviewed. The man had lost the hippocampus structures that transform short-term memory into long-term memory. Foer concludes that this man is incapable of any \\"stream of consciousness.\\" His life consistsonly of \\"droplets that evaporate.\\" He lives in a narrow spotlight, with darkness all around.<br /><br />Then Foer relates some of the characteristics of super-rememberers such as \\"Rainman\\" Kim Peek, one of the few people who seemed able to remember everything he read or encountered without notable use of standard mnemonic techniques. Foer contrasts this with the minds of the memory champs who befriended him in order to coach him to enter the U.S. and then the World Memory Championships.<br /><br />Foer covers a lot of ground in a readable, always entertaining way. The only major memory oddity he seems to have missed making mention of is that characterized by a few people, such as actress Marilu Henner, who have been featured in the news in recent years. These rarities have shown an ability to remember the headline events of any date you might rattle off to them - and to remember features of what they did that day - what they ate and what they wore. It would have been interesting if Foer had interviewed Henner or one of her kind, to find out if they just remember highlighted aspects of each day, or if they remember EVERYTHING.<br /><br />Then you might be interested in viewing the movie [[ASIN:B002B3ATBE Desk Set]] as a companion piece to this book. Katharine Hepburn plays a network researcher who is thrown up against the capabilities of a computer. In the course of proving the superiority of her mind over matter to Spencer Tracy, she smartly demonstrates some of the mnemonic techniques mentioned in \\"Moonwalking.\\"

0199267170|4.0|I fell for the Oxford edition and like the speaker of the Sonnets relative to the Dark Lady, I was blind to its defects too long.<br /><br />I bought two copies in 2005 when I was \\"rich\\" owing to the financial boom: the paperback for my teaching to be used in class, and the hardbound for my home study. I could see how relatively flimsy they are, for Oxford has long compensated for the flimsiness of its books with the prestige of its name, and the genuine scholarship it's put into editing. Oxford offered successively higher priced editions of its standards at the turn of the last century, from a flimsy blue buckram with India (very thin) paper to higher-priced editions in \\"Moroccan\\" leather, sporting gold leaf and more durable paper.<br /><br />Sure enough, the paperback King Lear disintegrated after being repeatedly copied and passed around, but not so much that I wasn't able to rescue the text of each play and staple it into a separate, handy little \\"Quarto\\" suitable for copying under \\"fair use\\" for students, and as an actor's prompt book.<br /><br />But while the hardbound endured at my home, I am now re-reading all of Shakespeare having read his complete works in 1962 as a lad of 12. I have Stage IV/D1 prostate cancer and the opportunity to live in a hospice style facility awaiting results from chemotherapy, and re-read Shakespeare in the light of modern scholarship, notably, the two versions of Lear (1608 in Quarto, 1623 in the Folio).<br /><br />This has exposed the relative and understandable flimsiness of the hardbound. It was made to be taken from the shelf and read at the desk. It can survive a Churchill chuckling and dozing over Falstaff and Timon of Athens in an easy chair with a cigar and whisky soda.<br /><br />But in a hospital room while I have my lunch is problematic for the book is being deformed past the limits set by its binders as it is distended in my bed, and I find that even small drops of food can remove the print from the opposite page when the pages are separated (I have no data as to the sticking-together action of another substance called \\"baby batter\\" but I imagine it isn't good.)<br /><br />Another problem is what I consider the \\"vanity\\", or perhaps self-filfilling, admission of a new play, Edward III, which may include passages by Shakespeare, but in which S, perhaps burned out at the time of Edward III on \\"history\\" plays, and not noted in what we know as exceptionally willing to put his nose to the grindstone, simply did not take an interest in the architecture of this history play.<br /><br />All the other history plays except for in part King John, Richard II and All Is True (Henry VIII), that is, the \\"core\\" plays from Henry IV Part 1 to Richard III, are layered pictures of England in the 15th century. In the \\"Henrician trilogy\\" S cannot resist all sorts of clever scenes, such as the Cade rebellion, Pucelle's nonsense, and Talbot v Mad Duchess and Crazed Dwarf.<br /><br />This is under better control in Richard III but only because the main character was an unusually dastardly person and therefore, given audience's eternal fascination with dastardly individuals, Richard can fill out the play in a way the non-dastardly Edward II could not. When Edward III goes at the Countess of Salisbury with wicked intent, the audience cheers him on, for wicked intent is theatrical, allowing the audience vicarious Thrills. But when Edward III stands down, wishing to be a virtuous ruler, we approve of this while stifling a yawn.<br /><br />This is just a mediaeval miniature snookered from Froissart. I fear that even at the zenith of their profession there was pressure - whether internal or external I don't know, to \\"discover\\" Edward III as a \\"Shakespeare\\" play even though it has lain, like Poe's Purloined Letter, in plain view for many years.<br /><br />Missing, or pressured out of Wells and Taylor in academic science worship of atomic facts, and a concomitant over-rating of details. A \\"Shakespeare\\" play  \\"must\\" contain the diversity of incident and depth of characterization we associate with Shakespeare otherwise it \\"must\\" by critical consensus anyway, be relegated with the numerous times we know Shakespeare was a \\"script doctor\\", always ready to help stuck authors with a script.<br /><br />Edward III is archtecturally tight as a drum but its chivalric military ethic is unrelieved by Shakespeare's questioning, thru Hotspur's psychotic exaggeration of the chivalric ideal (plucking bright honor out of the sea by her locks, etc.) thru its very absurdity and the effect it has on his wife. Or, for that matter, by Falstaff's speech on \\"Honour\\", Shakespeare's and Falstaff's obvious disgust with Prince John's dishonorable if bloodless victory in Henry IV Part 2. It's also hard to believe that Shakespeare's colleagues, or, the Master of the Revels, would have been able to \\"gag\\" Shakespeare on the issues raised in the canonical History plays given the success of his plays and the fact that questioning the chivalric ideal was no big deal, as far as we know, and that it was a frequent source of comedy, culminating, probably, in the \\"lost\\" play Cardenio.<br /><br />The fault of treating Edward III as canonical, however, is redeemed elsewhere, for following modern practice found in the Norton edition, Wells and Taylor include two version of King Lear, The History of King Lear and his History.<br /><br />Two polished versions of Lear exist. One came out in Quarto form in 1608 and featured a bit more excitement than the very different Folio version. The Quarto version features very interesting development of the character Albany until it is he, and not Edgar, who emerges as the natural leader of the end of the play, delivering the last and solemn lines:<br /><br />The weight of this sad time we must obey;<br />Speak what we feel, not what we ought to say.<br />The oldest hath borne most: we that are young<br />Shall never see so much, nor live so long.<br /><br />The Quarto version has also a more interesting revolt of Cornwall's servants and some Elizabethan tips on what to do for a gouged eye. Its scenes of reconciliation between Lear and Cordelia are a little more elaborate and plangent and were used in the definitive BBC version of Lear on video tape (and DVD now).<br /><br />But both are rewarding in their own way and both should be read to say \\"I have read King Lear\\".<br /><br />I am compelled to award this book a \\"four\\" because of its physical flimsiness as a paperback and the Edward III \\"canonization\\".t\\" contain the diversity of incident and depth of characterization we associate with Shakespeare otherwise it \\"must\\" by critical consensus anyway, be relegated with the numerous times we know Shakespeare was a \\"script doctor\\", always ready to help stuck authors with a script.<br /><br />Edward III is archtecturally tight as a drum but its chivalric military ethic is unrelieved by Shakespeare's questioning, thru Hotspur's psychotic exaggeration of the chivalric ideal (plucking bright honor out of the sea by her locks, etc.) thru its very absurdity and the effect it has on his wife. The Quarto version features very interesting development of the character Albany until it is he, and not Edgar, who emerges as the natural leader of the end of the play, delivering the last and solemn lines:<br /><br />The weight of this sad time we must obey;<br />Speak what we feel, not what we ought to say.<br />The oldest hath borne most: we that are young<br />Shall never see so much, nor live so long.<br /><br />The Quarto version hasalso a more interesting revolt of Cornwall's servants and some Elizabethan tips on what to do for a gouged eye. Its scenes of reconciliation between Lear and Cordelia are a little more elaborate and plangent and were used in the definitive BBC version of Lear on video tape (and DVD now).<br /><br />But both are rewarding in their own way and both should be read to say \\"I have read King Lear\\".<br /><br />I am compelled to award this book a \\"four\\" because of its physical flimsiness as a paperback and the Edward III \\"canonization\\".

0061950726|3.0|[[ASIN:0061950726 Orphan Train: A Novel]] is a touching novel with a common formula: an older isolated protagonist takes a young outcast under her wing.  This unlikely friendship provides a second chance to each character, and allows each of them to learn more about who they are.<br /><br />Molly Ayer is the younger character who is taken on to help Vivian sort through an attic full of momentos.  She is a young girl of Native American descent who has been knocked around the foster care system. She takes on the job at Vivian's to fulfill a community service obligation.  But she gains so much more than what she thought she would through the experience.<br /><br />Vivian is living out the twilight of her years in Maine, but as a young Irish orphan, Vivian was put on a train from NYC to the Midwest.  There is a historical basis to this story.  From the 1850s through the 1920s, orphan trains really did take kids from the East Coast to the farmlands.  Some were lucky and found good homes, but many were sent to a fate akin to slavery or indentured servitude.<br /><br />The sections written in Vivian's voice are mostly set in depression-era Minnesota.  Despite her heart-rending story, I found Vivian's plight to be fascinating.<br /><br />But the present day parts told from Molly's perspective were less compelling.  The author just seemed to have a better sense of Vivian's narrative voice.  Molly's character felt forced at times, with the author going to great lengths to try to portray her teenage angst.<br /><br />CONCLUSION<br /><br />This is a good book, and it is likely to find an audience.  But since the story is told from the perspective of these two characters, at times it becomes disjointed and almost reads like two separate novels.  Both perspectives are interesting and this book is a good read.  But it just isn't as cohesive as it could have been.  Nevertheless, this is recommended to anybody who has an interest in learning more about orphan trains.  And those who love coming of age novels will also likely enjoy this.love coming of age novels will also likely enjoy this.

B00B8XC2O0|4.0|J.J. Cale's five-disc box set, \\"Classic Album Selection\\" has been released via Mercury Records. The blurb tells you that this box set comes \\"in mini-LP paper sleeves, housed in an deluxe slipcase\\". What this mean means in English is that it comes in a stout cardboard casing with five card encased CDs. On this site the info claims \\"a new 2013 remastered collection of 5 classic studio albums\\" although the set bears no documentary support for this and in any case Cale's albums always sounded great. If any reader has any further information on where the \\"remastering\\" took place a comment below would be much appreciated. The box set includes five of Cale's classic albums: Naturally (1972), Troubadour (1976), Shades (1981), Grasshopper (1982) and #8 (1983). This by any standards is a very puzzling approach to choosing Cale's output for presentation. It lacks in chronology and begs the question why these five albums? Irritatingly it misses such classics as 1974s \\"Okie\\" easily one of his best albums (and thus no \\"Cajun Moon\\" or \\"I've got the same old blues\\") plus the excellent \\"Really\\" (1972). Similarly if Cale's 1979s album \\"5\\" had been added this would have grouped his first eight Mercury albums and surely not added much to the price bearing in mind that the Rhino 10 set box of Joni Mitchell's output from 1968 to 1979 came in cheaper per album. Frustrating is not the word for all this since it is hard to fathom the justifications for these arbitrary omissions which mean that the concept behind the set barely justifies 3 Stars.<br /><br />If on the other hand this is your introduction to the great man's music the songs on these five records speak for themselves with clarity and will introduce a sly old groove so contagious that you will need musical inoculation to free yourself from its vice like grip. Let us dispense immediately with the obligatory Eric Clapton and Dire Straits references and state emphatically that John Weldon Cale is a true original and needs neither of the former artists to define his or categorize music. Indeed if forced with a choice this reviewer would happily take the single instance of prime Cale his debut \\"Naturally\\" over the collected output of Eric Clapton since the demise of Derek and the Dominoes and some of the later bluster produced by Knopfler and Co. So then onto the music of Oklahoma's favourite son J.J. Cale -<br /><br />NATURALLY (1972) - A great debut album starting with the song immortalized by Lynyrd Skynyrd \\"Call me the breeze\\". It is a Cale manifesto so economical, laid-back and effortless with first class honours degree guitar picking and lyrics, which define the freedom of the great highway. It is easily matched by other highlights including the moody swamp blues of \\"Don't go to strangers\\" with a brilliant Cale vocal; wipe that tear from your eye with one of his best ballads \\"Magnolia\\" and recognise that the definitive version of \\"After Midnight: is to be located here. That said the standout out track on an album of stellar highlights is the pure country funk of the stunning \\"River runs deep\\"<br /><br />TROUBADOUR (1976) - A superb album which sees Cale's songs start to creep over the 3 minute mark and includes one that lasts over four! The album most famous track is the Cale signature tune \\"Cocaine\\" although even better is the great live favourite \\"Ride me high\\" which shows that Cale's only comparable rootsy white funk musical equivalent was the late lamented Lowell George. Seek out also the fabulous jazzy blues of \\"Hold on\\" and what amounts to a Cale hard rock song the pounding \\"I'm a gypsy man\\". Finally what can you say about \\"Travellin Light\\" with its great guitar work and hypnotic percussion? Granted there is a very good rocking Clapton cover on \\"Reptile\\" but the masters' version of this great song is unsurpassable and should be sought out by any Cale \\"virgins\\" as an immediate download.<br /><br />SHADES (1981) - For some this is viewed as a merely good Cale album as opposed to his usual meticulous standard. \\"Shades\\" is interesting for its more blues based approach to songwriting and for a little extra polish in the production. Repeated listens however reveal a little gem not least in the joy of the wistful \\"Wish I Had Not Said That\\", the rolling cover that is \\"Mama don't\\" and for those who want to know where the inspiration for Mark Knopfler's guitar style comes from should go no further than the superb opener \\"Carry on\\" which he later used to underpin his song \\"Border Reiver\\". Note also the present of two five minute plus instrumental songs the laidback blues of \\"Play my jack\\" a J.J. Cale master class in playing and the bluesy interplay of guitar and saxophone on \\"Cloudy Day\\"<br /><br />GRASSHOPPER (1982) - This is Cale settling down to his later groove with the ever so slightly Ry Cooder sounding \\"City Girls\\" and a real standout track and perennial favourite \\"Devil in disguise\\". Sadly it also contains this reviewers least favourite Cale songs the rather silly \\"Does your Mama like to reggae\\" which has 1980s mistake written all over it, equally the instrumental \\"Dr Jive\\" is throwaway stuff. Luckily the good dramatically outweighs the bad not least the porch acoustic picking of \\"Drifters wife\\" and the social comment of \\"Downtown LA\\" which almost cross-fertilizes across the Atlantic with Knopfler's \\"Down to the Waterline\\". While the he lovely piano ballad \\"You keep me hanging on\\" is a bit of a departure from the usual Cale guitar format its excellent all the same.<br /><br />#8 (1983) - There are parts of this album that lack the sparkle of earlier releases and sounds like Cale by numbers. In fact this is Cale's Reagan era social commentary album and in one sense was repeated for the Bush era by Ry Cooder in 2011's \\"Pull up some dust and sit down\\". The picture of Cale on the cover tells the story of album, which is one of the darkest and bleakest albums he has recorded. Song titles like \\"Loser\\", \\"People lie\\", \\"Unemployment\\" and the great duet with Christine Lakeland on \\"Money talks\\" create a mood of a Cale protest album. It is well worth a listen but not a completely essential part of the Cale canon of work. Interestingly he took a six-year recording hiatus after this album and probably avoided any danger (however slight0 of recording one of those horribly 1980s over produced high tech cursed high-end treble monstrosities.<br /><br />One reviewer has argued that \\"Cale is so objectively consistent that differences between albums in the long run boil down to which of the songs affect you on all days of the month and which of them affect you only on odd ones'. This is true but at the same time as the comments above show do not gain the impression that Cale's albums are a line of straight continuity without variance. The differences are subtle but they are differences all the same. J.J Cale remains one of the best recording artists of the past 40 plus years and all his albums either yield class and quality in parts or in an embarrassing abundance. The fact that he is probably best known from covers of his music by other musicians is something that sadly looks unlikely to change at this point in his long career. If this is to be the case treat him has your secret pleasure, with songs for every occasion and a nod tipped to the musical gods for placing one of their favourite sons amongst the midst of mere mortals.<br /><br />Postscript. Just heard the terribly sad news that J. J Cale died on 26th July 2013 from a heart attack. Saying he will be sorely missed barely covers the loss of such a great musician. Godspeed John.ual meticulous standard. \\"Shades\\" is interesting for its more blues based approach to songwriting and for a little extra polish in the production. Repeated listens however reveal a little gem not least in the joy of the wistful \\"Wish I Had Not Said That\\", the rolling cover that is \\"Mama don't\\" and for those who want to know where the inspiration for Mark Knopfler's guitar style comes from should go no further than the superb opener \\"Carry on\\" which he later used to underpin his song \\"Border Reiver\\". 006114665X|5.0|As the one-hundredth anniversary of the outbreak of World War I approaches we have seen many new books analyzing the reasons why conflict broke out in the summer of 1914.  Christopher Clark's The Sleepwalkers: How Europe Went To War In 1914, is certain to be ranked among the very best of these.  Clark takes the muddied waters and tangled threads of the multiple players in the tragedy and clarifies them in a series of thorough, heavily documented, and extremely well written chapters.<br /><br />Clark begins with a now largely forgotten coup: the overthrow and murder of the King and Queen of Serbia and the installation of a new King and dynasty in 1903.  This is appropriate, because that event set Serbia on the road toward territorial aggrandizement and closer Russian ties. This in turn weakened the Ottoman Empire, destabilized the Balkans, and led Russia and Austria-Hungary into one confrontation after another.  Those confrontations inevitably led to Germany, France, and Great Britain becoming much more heavily invested in Balkan disputes than they had ever intended to be and increased the over all tension level throughout Europe.<br /><br />Clark then turns to a closer examination of the diplomatic situation in Europe during the last twenty five years or so before 1914.  He traces the polarization of Europe as Germany and Britain's formerly warm relations began to cool, France and Russia forged an unlikely alliance, and the growing weakness of the Ottoman Empire caused European countries like Italy, Russia, and Austria-Hungary to seek new territories and concessions.  Throughout this section Clark emphasizes that there were a number of universal and dangerous misconceptions: that one's own nation was gradually getting weaker and needed to go to war while it still had a chance to win, or that a nation's enemies were secretly working together to gain critical advantages, or that Russia was far more powerful than she actually was.  Clark has a good eye for an illuminating anecdote, such as the ones detailing the weakness of the Austrian Minister Conrad von Hotzendorf for a pretty lady. There are also some eerie echoes of our own time, such as Serbian \\"ethnic cleansing\\" or Italian air strikes on Libya.<br /><br />The last segment deals with the actual crisis that led to war in 1914, beginning with the assassination of Archduke Franz Ferdinand and his morganatic wife at Sarajevo.  Here the reader is struck by the tragic coincidences that over and over again seemed to work to make war more likely: the distraction of the French press and public by a sensational trial involving senior government officials, or the inopportune state visit of France's President to the Tsar of Russia, or the way diplomats and generals kept making dangerous and thoughtless maneuvers as if they were of no more consequence than moves on a chess board.  Eventually, without anyone really meaning to do it, what had been a little local affair exploded into a continent wide war, leaving many leaders shaking their heads and wondering how it had all happened.<br /><br />The world today is far more dangerous than it was in 1914, and it is important that books like this, with ample warnings for us of the hazards of brinksmanship, extreme nationalism, hubris, and lack of imagination. President John F. Kennedy famously kept Barbara Tuchman's The Guns of August on his desk in the Oval Office as a reminder of how quickly events can spiral out of control.  I think The Sleepwalkers deserves just as much respect today.ing anecdote, such as the ones detailing the weakness of the Austrian Minister Conrad von Hotzendorf for a pretty lady. There are also some eerie echoes of our own time, such as Serbian \\"ethnic cleansing\\" or Italian air strikes on Libya.<br /><br />The last segment deals with the actual crisis that led to war in 1914, beginning with the assassination of Archduke Franz Ferdinand and his morganatic wife at Sarajevo.  Here the reader is struck by the tragic coincidences that over and over again seemed to work to make war more likely: the distraction of the French press and public by a sensational trial involving senior government officials, or the inopportune state visit of France's President to the Tsar of Russia, or the way diplomats and generals kept making dangerous and thoughtless maneuvers as if they were of no more consequence than moves on a chess board.  Eventually, without anyone really meaning to do it, what had been a little local affair exploded into a continent wide war, leaving many leaders shaking their heads and wondering how it had all happened.<br /><br />The world today is far more dangerous than it was in 1914, and it is important that books like this, with ample warnings for us of the hazards of brinksmanship, extreme nationalism, hubris, and lack of imagination. President John F. Kennedy famously kept Barbara Tuchman's The Guns of August on his desk in the Oval Office as a reminder of how quickly events can spiral out of control.  I think The Sleepwalkers deserves just as much respect today.

B007KQI1NA|4.0|This unit gets usable signals inside my house, through a metal roof and siding. It is good to start near a window though so it can get initial lock on a few satellites before trying to receive the weak ones.  Houses with non-metal roofs should be no problem at all.  I think the WAAS SBAS also works (it needs to be outside with a view of the GEO satellites to the south) but not positive of that.<br /><br />The main problem I've been having is that the baud rate never changes from 4800 despite attempts to reset it.  This means only NMEA mode is useful.  In binary mode, the data is garbled since 4800 baud cannot keep up with the number of bytes sent.  Configuring gpsd to start with the -b option prevents the receiver from being switched to binary mode-- then positions can be received in NMEA mode.  If you find it was inadvertently switched to binary mode you can use 'gpsctl -f -n /dev/ttyUSB0' to reset to NMEA (kill gpsd if running first).<br /><br />Also the cable is too short.  It works fine with a 15 foot extension though that is not officially legal USB.

I always clicked through to it, read the synopsis, and thought no thanks. An able-bodied person falling in love with a quadriplegic? One, sad. Two, yeah right.<br /><br />I have to say, I was totally and completely wrong. Stunning. That's how I would describe this book. I'm amazed at how quickly I was drawn into the characters' lives. Told from the point of view of Lou, the down-on-her-luck caretaker of Will, a surly quadriplegic with nothing but disdain for the world around him. Lou soon discovers the real reason she has been assigned this job - keeping Will from coming to self harm before a pre-set date, when he will make the decision to take his own life. Lou uses the time she has left to change his mind, and every step of the way you are hoping along with her that it will work.<br /><br />Lou is endearing and sweet while never veering into nauseating. Will is surly and moody but never unlikeable. I mean, can you blame him? His parents are both deeply flawed and yet admirable in their love for their son. Lou's family is also imperfect, but they were so human that you could forgive them. Every detail of this story, every character and setting, was nuanced and multi-dimensional.<br /><br />Just... perfection. While this book is slightly political (without ever being preachy), it's really just a story about humanity, which at its core revolves around love. In the past year, three books I have read have gotten a 5-star rating. I don't dole these things out lightly. So give it a shot.

0807014273|4.0|Years ago, I read Thomas Kenneally's novel, Schindler's Ark [later used for the movie Schindler's List]. In the book, I recall a conversation in which two prisoners discussed suicide. One of them said, 'Don't do that, you will never know how it ends.' I thought of this conversation when I read Man's Search for Meaning.<br /><br />I saw this book in a Christian bookshop display. According to the cover, it has sold 9 million copies - a massive bestseller, yet I had never heard of it. The bookseller told me that there was little demand for it generally but it always sold well when he put it on open display in the shop.<br /><br />The writer, Viktor E Frankl, was a Jewish psychiatrist from Vienna who was incarcerated at various Nazi concentration camps during the Second World War. During this period, he was employed as a road builder and medical orderly and did not suffer the ultimate fate of extermination in a gas chamber unlike his wife and parents [his sister had mercifully been able to emigrate to Australia before the War started]. The main part of the book concerns Frankl's experience as a prisoner - a story told in a matter of fact quite unjudgemental way. There are moments of reflection and often grim gallows humour but little bitterness or resentment about the sheer injustice at his treatment - the prisoners were after all innocent men and women.<br /><br />During his imprisonment, he noticed that many of his fellow inmates who were not destined for execution still gave up life through depression, suicide and typhus - indeed for many, extermination was a release. Paradoxically, these people were often the more sturdy prisoners. The idea came to Frankl in a discussion with another prisoner who, while dressed in filthy ill fitting prison clothes wondered what his wife would think of him dressed like this. Frankl considered the same thing and the constant thought of his wife [both the memories of their pre-War life together and speculation on her present state] kept him in a more positive state. It made him realise that if people have a life purpose and meaning, it can give them strength to survive whatever misfortunes they may experience in life. He quotes Nietzsche, 'He who has a why to live can survive any how'.<br /><br />This philosophy informed Frankl's post-War psychiatric work in which he developed a field called logotherapy, in which patients get over neuroses they may suffer through developing an appreciation of their lives' meaning. I found this an interesting topic that has made me more appreciative of what I have in life and the experiences I have had. The chapter in which he explains it, 'Logotherapy in a Nutshell' was perhaps a bit hard to read but it well worth reflecting on.<br /><br />Very good book.

B009EJSUBY|5.0|The short answer is very good indeed. The full answer takes a little longer.<br /><br />Like many veteran listeners I never took Van Cliburn seriously.  I mean all that publicity, the competition, his rather pretentious, \\"cultivated\\" speaking style, etc. The critics weren't very positive about him either. As a result, the vast majority of these recordings are new to me. But now that all of his recordings have been reissued it's time for an assessment. So here goes.<br /><br />It's not accurate to say that Cliburn's recordings are paricularly uneven. Cliburn was a remarkably consistent pianist. In fact, you could say he played eveything more or less the same way (which I'll describe later). However, some works just don't respond well to the Cliburn Treatment. For example, when intimacy or atmosphere are required (as in, Clair de lune-- and in fact just about all of Debussy--Brahms' op 117/1 intermezzo, the first movement of the Moonlight Sonata) he seems wooden and ill at ease. In other cases (like the Brahms First Concerto) he plays as if the piece were by Rachmaninoff. But most of the works in this set fit him perfectly.<br /><br />Here are the main characteristics of Cliburn's playing.<br /><br />1.  Tone.  I've never heard any other pianist sound like this.  Only Rubinstein is even close. His sound is so large and rich it just fills the entire room. It's not a matter of volume; it's the sheer amplitude of his sound. He is incapable of producing an ugly sound on the piano. Nope, couldn't do it if he tried. You might argue that sometimes (as in the first movement of the Prokofiev Sixth) an ugly sound is required. But Cliburn is Cliburn, and the movement sounds positively gorgeous in his performance. Even when he sounds disengaged (as in the Chopin First Concerto) he is a pleasure to listen to. It should also be said that Cliburn rarely plays really softly. There are very few wispy pianissimos here (rather like Rubinstein). The tone is full and rounded at all dynamic levels.<br /><br />2.  Style. Looking around desperately for some adjective to decribe Cliburn's general approach, I came up with two: \\"stately\\" and declamatory.\\" His playing is very \\"public\\" (hence the problem with intimate music) and totally unruffled. He plays to the upper balcony. Tempi are often somewhat slower than usual, and he rarely speeds up to create excitement. Instead, the huge sound gets even more huge, and one is overwhelmed by the sheer size and amplitude. In fact, aside from all that publicity, it's hard to understand why he so excited the public. He's not a particularly \\"exciting\\" player (which is not to say he wasn't a great one). It's the grandeur of his playing that most impresses me.<br /><br />3. Clarity.  You want to hear each and every note of the piano part of Brahms' First?  Or Rachy 3?  Cliburn's your man. This clarity, combined with the steadiness of his rhythm, can sometime impart a \\"notey\\" (if such a word exists) quality to his playing.  Most often, though, the clarity and stateliness makes his playing uniquely \\"grand.\\"  ( I wonder if the great masters of the Golden Age--Cortot or Rachmaninoff for axample--sounded like this. We'll never know from the sound on their discs.)<br /><br />As for the specific performances, most of them are simply superb. Of the concerto recordings, the ones with the Chicago Symphony--Beethoven 4 and 5, Prokofiev 3 and MacDowell, Schumann, Rachy 2) are all great performances. The Brahms 2 is also pretty good. The performances with Philadelphia are not nearly on this level. The Chopin 1, Grieg, Lizst, Rachy Rhapsody all sound generic to me. The Tchaikovsky (perhaps the most famous classical music recording ever issued) is excellent, but the live Rachy 3 is a mess as a recording. (I have to admit there are very few pianists--Byron Janis one one of them, Cliburn isn't another--who can make this work sound like anything but junk to me. Some highlights: The superlative Chopin Sonatas, The liszt Sonata, Brahms Handel Variations are just a few of the treasures.<br /><br />Cliburn generally received excellent sound, which, given the beauty of his tone, is very important.  Most of these recordings are superb, paricularly the \\"Living Sound\\" recordings, which are often quite spectacular (listen, for eaxample to the Prokofiev concerto, which is breathtakingly clear and impactive). The recordings with the Philadelphia are much less impressive.<br /><br />These are, of course, cd \\"duplications\\" of the original lps, so recoding time is limited.  I found that oddly nostalgic.  At times I wanted to get up and turn the record over.  Don't try to read the liner notes without a magnifying glass.<br /><br />Cliburn had his ups and downs, but this set makes it clear that he was a really great pianist.

B00C5ZORES|3.0|Well, after hearing that Fall Out Boy had returned after a hiatus for several years, I was intrigued to hear what they had to offer.  I used to listen to these guys very fervently throughout high school and my early college years prior to their hiatus, and so I figured I'd give this album a shot.<br /><br />Ultimately, I must say that this album is a mix of tragedy and triumph.  On the tragic side of things, Fall Out Boy seems to have been so eager to seek maturity that their trademark sound has been largely thrown aside.  Instead, it appears that FOB has decided to take on a more R&B/pop sound for much of their newer material.  In many of the tracks, the production is too shiny, taking away from the band's authentic sound and talent, and the lyricism and musicianship appears to have taken on a more simplistic structure.  In short, some of these tracks may feel more at home on one of Usher's older albums rather than on Fall Out Boy's newest effort.<br /><br />However, there is also a sense of triumph here.  Patrick Stump's vocals have never sounded better, and the instrumentation is still good, even if it is not up to the level of their older material.  Also, Fall Out Boy has shown that they still have essence to them, and has not burned out or become stale like many of their pop-punk/emo-pop peers (Paramore, The All-American Rejects, Boys Like Girls, I'm looking right at you).  The songs \\"The Phoenix\\" and \\"My Songs Know What You Did in the Dark\\" are strong, fun, catchy tracks, and I could listen to them over and over again.  It was also pretty cool of them to feature Elton John on the closer of the album.<br /><br />So, ultimately, I have mixed feelings.  While most of the album's tracks make for decent or adequate listens, only the first two (and to some degree the closer) are true standouts in comparison to their older, more pop-punk/emo-pop-centered material.  But Fall Out Boy still has shown that they can keep some essence to them, and has managed to run aheadof most of the pop-punk/emo-pop pack.<br /><br />Final Rating: 3.5/5

0374214913|3.0|I really enjoy books that can bend genre into something entirely new. I've read several novels that manage to do this remarkably well, Robert Jackson Bennett's The Troupe and Audrey Niffenegger's The Time Traveler's Wife both spring immediately to mind. So when I read the blurb for Robin Sloan's Mr. Penumbra's 24 Hour Book Store I had high hopes that I had found another such novel, something I could recommend to my non-genre reading friends that might bridge the gap between their tastes and mine. Sadly, despite showing a lot of promise in it's premise and characterization, Sloan's novel seems to be a bit too clever for its own good and tuned out to be something of a disappointment.<br /><br />The synopsis from the publisher is as follows:<br /><br />The Great Recession has shuffled Clay Jannon out of his life as a San Francisco Web-design drone--and serendipity, sheer curiosity, and the ability to climb a ladder like a monkey has landed him a new gig working the night shift at Mr. Penumbra's 24-Hour Bookstore. But after just a few days on the job, Clay begins to realize that this store is even more curious than the name suggests. There are only a few customers, but they come in repeatedly and never seem to actually buy anything, instead \\"checking out\\" impossibly obscure volumes from strange corners of the store, all according to some elaborate, long-standing arrangement with the gnomic Mr. Penumbra. The store must be a front for something larger, Clay concludes, and soon he's embarked on a complex analysis of the customers' behavior and roped his friends into helping to figure out just what's going on. But once they bring their findings to Mr. Penumbra, it turns out the secrets extend far outside the walls of the bookstore.<br />My disappointment aside, there are some interesting things going on in Sloan's story. The protagonist, Clay, certainly typifies the over-educated, inexperienced twenty-somethings that have suffered from the economic downturn and find themselves adrift with little sense of direction. Clay's dissatisfaction with his life's trajectory is palpable, and his decision to throw his energy into the mysteries of Penumbra's bookstore and its odd collection of clients seems like the act of a man desperate for something to grab hold of. The romantic subplot suffers from a similar sense of desperation, where Clay is content to allow Kat to run hot and cold while he simply drifts about her in a loose orbit awaiting her pleasure. It might not be the most fulfilling plot line, but the authenticity of its architecture is undeniable. The banter between the cast is easy and natural and full of pop culture references that genre readers will find engaging. As a character study, Mr. Penumbra's 24 Hour Bookstore is a success. It's only when his plot starts intersecting with his big idea, that some holes become apparent.<br /><br />While going to great lengths to provide an air of mystery to the true purpose of the bookstore and the mission of Penumbra and his acolytes, Sloan fails to deliver much more than some compelling stage dressing. The puzzles are far too easily solved, with out much in the way of actual cost to any of the protagonists. The juxtaposition of ancient methodology versus the technological path is obviously the big idea that Sloan is fixated on, but that comparison, while interesting doesn't provide enough real conflict to the plot. Sloan seems to be saying why can't we all just get along, but gives little real reason that we should do so.<br /><br />Sloan makes some excellent observations about the modern world's dependence on technology. For example, readers are treated to an awkward virtual date between our protagonist and his love interest, and an otherwise brilliant character cannot operate something as low tech as a copy of the New York Times. But rather than make any real statement about how technology may not be the cure all for all the challenges faced by the protagonist in his search to unlock themysterious knowledge that Penumbra seeks, Sloan simply allows technology to provide the answer time and time again. While real paper books do play a role in the eventual uncovering of the grand mystery that Penumbra and Gannon's band of investigators, most of the riddles Sloan presents are too conveniently solved with the aid of modern technology. Dues ex machina play far to great a role in the solving of problems. Need to copy a book in a secret library with incredible security, look no further than the Internet for plans for a an easily smuggled scanner made of cardboard and portable cameras. Can't decipher the book, well why not have the vast computing power of Google at your disposal. Answers come far too easily to Sloan's cast, and because of that there is little satisfaction when the ultimate goal is achieved.<br /><br />Make no mistake, Mr. Penumbra's 24 Hour Bookstore is a quick, light and ultimately enjoyable read. But with so strong a premise, I expected far more than I received. Those looking for something with a little more substance and less magical hand waving should stick to Robert Jackson Bennett and the like. There's little water beneath Sloan's albeit appealing mirage.

A matcher is one that \\"keeps score\\" making sure that their reciprocity is matched on a tit for tat basis.<br /><br />So the question is which style is most successful.  While we tend to think that takers are more successful, the studies show something very interesting.  Over a long term, certain groups of givers achieve greater levels of success than takers.  But not all givers - certain givers rank near the bottom on any measure of success.<br /><br />Adam Grant, the author, has written a very interesting and highly insightful book that looks into the differences between givers, takers and matchers.  In his book, he explores what makes certain givers more successful.  He also goes into great detail about why certain givers experience burn out and why they fail to enjoy the success that other givers achieve.<br /><br />Mr. Grant is a professor at the Wharton School of Business. The book is well written in a very conversational style.  It is filled with numerous stories based on psychological studies.
